Yuen dynasty. This only embraces equations of one unknown quantity
which is always denominated V'ien u heaven,** but the equations are
unlimited in the number of degrees, and this forms a most remarkable
distinction, in advance not only of the Hindoo, Greek, and Arabic Alge-
bra, but also of the European science, until a comparatively recent period.
The method of solving equations of all orders, discovered by Horner in
1819, and which has s〇 greatly facilitated this branch of Arithmetic in
the West, is found identical in Chinese works written 600 years previous,
stated and illustrated with great perspicuity; and it is a matter of sur-
prise to native mathematicians to learn that this is a recent discovery in
Europe. The scheme invented by Hariot, of placing all the significant
terms of an equation on one side, and which is considered the foundation
of some most important branches of the science in Europe, was employed
by the Chinese from the first. There are many other points worth re-
marking, but they are beyond the limits of this notice.
Contemporaneous with the above-named author, or nearly so, another
work appeared by Choo She-ke, called 祕 四元玉鑑左祕 2
“Precious mirror of the Four monads,” giving a development of tlie
method of quadriliteral equations, in which the four quantities, either
known or unknown, are named 谷 天之’谷 ew “地纪
““ 人《/如 “man，” 魏 物魏 A “thing.”
While the cumbrous category of symbols employed in the Hindoo Al-
gebra must have given an unwieldiness to their operations, the Chinese
in avoiding that inconvenience, by the adoption of certain positions in the
place of symbols, deprived themselves of the peculiar elasticity that cha-
racterizes the modern complexion of the science. Both the one and the
other however possessed an evident superiority over the Arabians, who
were entirely destitute of symbols, and expressed the idea of unknown
quantities, positive and negative terms, names of operation, &c. by words
and phrases at full length.
An expression taken from a Hindoo Algebraical work, the commentary
on the Vija-ganita,* dated 1463, with its equivalent according to the an-
cient Chinese and modern European methods, will give some idea of the
character of the respective notations.
Hindoo.
ya vv \ ya gh 6 yav 5 ya 12 ru 4
Chinese.
* From Colebrooke's translation, page 219.


TRANSLATION OF DE MORGAN’S ALGEBRA.
Authors who have undertaken to investigate the history of Algebra,
have traced it through Italy, to the Arabian mathematicians who flourish-
ed about the Bth century of our era; and these there is good reason to
believe obtained their knowledge originally from the Hindoos. Through
the labours of Colebrooke and other Oriental scholars, the European pub-
lic have been made acquainted with the Hindoo system of Algebra, and
the various branches of mathematics cultivated by that people; and the
refinement to which they were able to carry their methods of
at a very remote period, bas established their reputation in connexion with
the exact sciences; for it appears the Arabs never attained to that degree
of excellence that characterises the works of their Indian precursors. It
is a fact worth noticing also, that the Chinese were in possession of a sys-
tem of Algebra, at least as early as the first introduction of the science
into Europe, but unfortunately the data are insufficient to throw much
light regarding the source whence they obtained it.
We know that so early at least as the sth or 6th centuries, when Bud-
dhism had gained a footing in this empire, there was an exchange of li-
terature between the Chinese and Hindoos ; the Chinese book catalogues
of that period, contain notices of a number of Indian works on mathema-
tics and astronomy, and there is still extant a translation of a Hindoo sys--
tem of mathematical chronology, entitled c 九執歴 cAzA Ze 认, which*
was used in China at the beginning of the Bth century.
The Mohammedans also who entered the empire in considerable num-
bers during the Y-uen dynasty, doubtless brought some of the Arabian
and Persian science with them, but we have no precise information as to
any of their national works being translated into Chinese. After the over-
throw of the Mongolian power, the first emperor of the Ming made an
effort to have the principal of the Arabic works which fell into his pos-
session put into Chinese, by a commission formed of Chinese and Arabic
scholars, but the result of their labours has not survived to the present
time, although we have in the Ming history a complete system of Moham-*
medan chronology, as it was used during that dynasty.
While these facts shew the possibility of the Chinese having obtained
their Algebra from a foreign source, something further is still wanted to
complete the evidence that they actually did so. It is true we find many
points of analogy between the Chinese and Hindoo methods, and yet there
is such a striking distinction in some respects, as fully to vindicate the
claim of the former to originality of inventive resource, if not to actual
priority in the initial phase of this new science.
The Chinese name for the system used by them, J 立天元 JSei.A 《’細 z
yuen u Employing the Celestial monad and the principal ancient work
on the subject, is 2 侧圆海錢 2， 牙， by L》Yay of the


In this expression the lower unit is ft 2; in the next line above, the left
hand 2 represents 2ab ; that on the right is 2ac ; the left hand unit in the
middle line is and the right hand one c 2 ; the 2 standing diagonally
under this line marks the product of the two symbols right and left, or
2&c ; and the other diagonal number above represents the product of the
upper and lower symbols or 2ax ; and so on.
In the 17th century, Algebra as it then existed in Europe, was intro-
duced into China by some of the Jesuit missionaries, under the title @
报方 々挪/似设， literally “Borrowing roots and powers;’’ but this
never seems to have been adopted to any extent by the 一 fact
which is satisfactorily explained by the obvious superiority to their own
system.
The proficiency which many of the natives have attained to m the use
of the is a su 伍 cient evidence of their aptitude to grapple
with the more refined branches of mathematics; while several of their re-
cent works show very clearly the great disadvantage they labour under,
for want of a pliable notation. The present translation was undertaken
with a view to relieve them from this difficulty, by putting them in pos-
session of a simple method, by which they may develope their mathe-
matical ideas; these being often very imperfectly stated, in consequence
of the insufficiency of the methods they are obliged to adopt. The cha-
racter which Algebra has now assumed in Europe, is of such a totally
different cast from what it was two centuries back, that it may be looked
upon as a new science; and it has been thought advisable, instead of
using the old name Tseay kan to give it a new designation more in
accordance w 丨 th its altered aspect; the s 代數摹 sod Ago “ Sub-
stitutionary arithmetic M has been adopted accordingly.
A simpler treatise than that of De Morgan's might no doubt have been
selected, had the object been merely to initiate the learner into tlie me-
chanical process of Algebraic manipulation ; but in view of the disposi-
tion shewn by native mathematicians to investigate principles and deduce
consequences, it is probable that a work of this character will really do
more to advance the ultimate study of the science, than many books of
mere formal rules; besides being a suitable preparation for a treatise on
the Differential calculus, wliich will be issued about the same time.
A. WYLIE,
Shanghae, October, 1859.

The terms ya v v &c. in the Hindoo notation are the initials of words;
i. e. ya 夕 夕 “quantum,” the sign of the unknown quantity, »
the 4th power, gh the cube, v the square, and ru for rupa u absolute
number.” The coefficient is placed against every term in the expression,
even although it should only be a unit, and a negative is shewn by a
point over the last figure; when a term in the series is wanting, it has
still the initial letters, with the coefficient 0. In this last respect there is
a dose analogy with the Chinese，who however dispense with the sym-
bols， by placing each coefficient in its proper position in the column， and
when any term is wanting, the place in the column is supplied by a ci-
plier. The position of absolute number is signified by the character
fae placed by the side ; but this is by qo means the limit of the series,
which admits of being extended upwards or downwards from that point
ad infinitum, every step upwards being an additional power, and the se-
veral steps downwards being (not roots, but) unity divided by the suc-
cessive powers. A negative is signified by a diagonal stroke drawn
through the number. To multiply or divide the whole expression by so
many powers, is merely to remove tbe character fae up or down the co-
lumn so many stages.
In the Sze yuen or Quadriliteral algebra, there is an evident advance
upon the original idea, and in the notion of using symbols to represent
known numbers, the Chinese have again anticipated us by more than two
centuries. There are however disadvantages connected with this mode
of operation from which the T'een yuen is free. The method of notation
is to place the coefficients of the four symbols round the absolute number
in the form of a cross, from which the successive powers radiate towards
the cardinal points. The four simple monads are represented 一 :一
